Immigrant parents, in particular, may face challenges in navigating the complex systems and policies of their new country. By involving these parents in community engagement activities, they can gain a better understanding of their rights and responsibilities, and feel more empowered to advocate for themselves and their families. This can lead to improved social equity and integration within the community.



#### 12.3b Importance of Inclusive Community Engagement



In order for community engagement to truly promote social equity, it must be inclusive and involve all members of the community, including those who may be traditionally marginalized or excluded. This means actively seeking out and including the voices of low-income residents, people of color, immigrants, and other underrepresented groups.



One way to ensure inclusive community engagement is through the use of participatory action research (PAR). PAR involves community members in all stages of the research process, from identifying the problem to implementing solutions. This approach not only empowers community members, but also ensures that their perspectives and needs are taken into account.



In addition, community engagement should also involve education and capacity building. This means providing resources and training to community members so they can effectively participate in decision-making processes and advocate for their needs. By building the capacity of community members, they can become more active and engaged citizens, leading to a more equitable and inclusive community.



#### 12.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Social Equity



There are many successful examples of community engagement leading to improved social equity in brownfield redevelopment projects. One such example is the redevelopment of the former Cabrini-Green public housing project in Chicago. The redevelopment process involved extensive community engagement, including the formation of a resident council and the inclusion of community members on the redevelopment planning committee. This led to the creation of a mixed-income community that better met the needs and desires of the residents.



Another successful case is the redevelopment of the former Bethlehem Steel site in Baltimore, Maryland. The project involved extensive community engagement, including the formation of a community advisory board and the use of PAR methods. This led to the creation of a community-driven redevelopment plan that prioritized affordable housing, job opportunities, and environmental remediation. As a result, the project has been praised for its inclusive and equitable approach.



In conclusion, community engagement is a crucial component of promoting social equity in brownfield redevelopment projects. By actively involving and empowering community members, we can create more inclusive and equitable communities that meet the needs and desires of all residents. Through inclusive community engagement and the use of participatory methods, we can ensure that marginalized voices are heard and that all members of the community have a say in the future of their neighborhoods.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and social equity. We have seen how the redevelopment of contaminated land can have a significant impact on the surrounding community, both positive and negative. We have also discussed the importance of considering social equity in the planning and implementation of brownfield redevelopment projects.



One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the need for community involvement and engagement in the brownfield redevelopment process. By involving community members in decision-making and planning, we can ensure that their voices are heard and their needs are taken into account. This can help to address any potential social equity issues that may arise from the redevelopment project.



Another important aspect to consider is the potential displacement of low-income and marginalized communities due to gentrification. It is crucial to have policies and strategies in place to mitigate this displacement and ensure that these communities are not further marginalized by the redevelopment project.



Overall, brownfield redevelopment can be a powerful tool for promoting social equity and revitalizing communities. However, it must be approached with careful consideration and a commitment to involving and uplifting all members of the community.



### Exercises

#### Exercise 1: Community Engagement

Think about a brownfield redevelopment project in your local area. How was the community involved in the planning and decision-making process? Were there any social equity concerns raised by community members? How were these concerns addressed?



#### Exercise 2: Gentrification and Displacement

Research a case study of a brownfield redevelopment project that resulted in gentrification and displacement of low-income communities. What were the social equity implications of this project? How could these issues have been mitigated or avoided?



#### Exercise 3: Environmental Justice

Discuss the concept of environmental justice and its relationship to brownfield redevelopment. How can we ensure that the benefits and burdens of these projects are distributed equitably among all members of the community?



#### Exercise 4: Policy Analysis

Analyze the policies and regulations in your local area related to brownfield redevelopment and social equity. Are there any gaps or areas for improvement? How could these policies be strengthened to better address social equity concerns?



#### Exercise 5: Stakeholder Mapping

Create a stakeholder map for a hypothetical brownfield redevelopment project. Identify all stakeholders involved, including community members, government agencies, developers, and environmental organizations. How could these stakeholders work together to promote social equity in the project?





## Chapter: Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment



### Introduction:



In recent years, there has been a growing concern about the impact of industrialization and urbanization on the environment. As a result, there has been a push towards sustainable development and the remediation of contaminated sites, known as brownfields. Brownfield redevelopment is the process of revitalizing and reusing abandoned or underutilized properties that have been contaminated by hazardous substances. This chapter will focus on the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and environmental justice, exploring the challenges and opportunities that arise when addressing both environmental and social issues.



Brownfield redevelopment projects often face significant challenges, including financial, legal, and technical barriers. These challenges are compounded when considering environmental justice, which seeks to address the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. Environmental justice advocates argue that these communities are often the most affected by brownfield sites and should have a say in the redevelopment process. This chapter will examine the role of environmental justice in brownfield redevelopment and the importance of community involvement in these projects.



The chapter will also discuss the various strategies and approaches used in brownfield redevelopment, such as risk assessment, remediation, and reuse planning. These strategies must consider not only the environmental impacts but also the potential social and economic benefits for the surrounding community. Additionally, the chapter will explore the role of government policies and regulations in promoting sustainable and equitable brownfield redevelopment.



Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of brownfield redevelopment and its intersection with environmental justice. It will highlight the challenges and opportunities in addressing contaminated sites while promoting social and environmental justice. By understanding the complexities of brownfield redevelopment, we can work towards creating sustainable and equitable communities for all.





### Section: 13.1 Environmental Justice and Brownfields:



Environmental justice is a critical aspect of brownfield redevelopment, as it seeks to address the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. These communities, often low-income and minority populations, are more likely to live near contaminated sites and suffer from the negative health and environmental impacts. Therefore, it is essential to define environmental justice and understand its role in brownfield redevelopment.



#### 13.1a Definition of Environmental Justice



Environmental justice is a social movement that aims to address environmental injustice, which is the exposure of poor and marginalized communities to harm from hazardous waste, resource extraction, and other land uses from which they do not receive benefits. It is a concept that encompasses distributive, procedural, and recognition justice. Distributive justice refers to the equitable distribution of environmental risks and benefits, while procedural justice focuses on the fair and meaningful participation of all stakeholders in decision-making processes. Recognition justice recognizes the oppression and difference in environmental justice communities and seeks to address them. Additionally, the capacity of these communities to convert social goods into a flourishing society is also a crucial aspect of environmental justice.



The United States Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) defines environmental justice as "the fair treatment and meaningful involvement of all people regardless of race, color, national origin, or income, with respect to the development, implementation, and enforcement of environmental laws, regulations, and policies." This definition highlights the importance of including all stakeholders, especially those from marginalized communities, in environmental decision-making processes. It also recognizes the historical and systemic injustices faced by these communities and the need for fair treatment and meaningful involvement in addressing environmental issues.



Environmental justice is also discussed as environmental racism or environmental inequality. These terms highlight the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on communities of color and low-income populations. Studies have shown that these communities are more likely to live near contaminated sites and suffer from the negative health and environmental impacts. Therefore, environmental justice seeks to address these inequalities and promote fair treatment and meaningful involvement of all stakeholders in environmental decision-making processes.



Joan Martinez-Alier's concept of the environmentalism of the poor is also crucial in understanding environmental justice. It highlights the ways in which marginalized communities, particularly those in the Global South, are disproportionately affected by environmental degradation. It also emphasizes the importance of including their perspectives and needs in environmental decision-making processes. Martinez-Alier's work also introduces the concept of "ecological distribution conflicts," which are conflicts over access to and control of natural resources and the resulting environmental impacts. These conflicts are often rooted in social and economic inequalities and must be addressed in the pursuit of environmental justice.



In conclusion, environmental justice is a critical aspect of brownfield redevelopment as it seeks to address the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. It encompasses distributive, procedural, and recognition justice and highlights the importance of including all stakeholders in decision-making processes. The EPA's definition of environmental justice and Martinez-Alier's concept of the environmentalism of the poor provide a comprehensive understanding of this concept and its role in brownfield redevelopment. 





### Section: 13.1 Environmental Justice and Brownfields:



Environmental justice is a critical aspect of brownfield redevelopment, as it seeks to address the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. These communities, often low-income and minority populations, are more likely to live near contaminated sites and suffer from the negative health and environmental impacts. Therefore, it is essential to define environmental justice and understand its role in brownfield redevelopment.



#### 13.1a Definition of Environmental Justice



Environmental justice is a social movement that aims to address environmental injustice, which is the exposure of poor and marginalized communities to harm from hazardous waste, resource extraction, and other land uses from which they do not receive benefits. It is a concept that encompasses distributive, procedural, and recognition justice. Distributive justice refers to the equitable distribution of environmental risks and benefits, while procedural justice focuses on the fair and meaningful participation of all stakeholders in decision-making processes. Recognition justice recognizes the oppression and difference in environmental justice communities and seeks to address them. Additionally, the capacity of these communities to convert social goods into a flourishing society is also a crucial aspect of environmental justice.



The United States Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) defines environmental justice as "the fair treatment and meaningful involvement of all people regardless of race, color, national origin, or income, with respect to the development, implementation, and enforcement of environmental laws, regulations, and policies." This definition highlights the importance of including all stakeholders, especially those from marginalized communities, in environmental decision-making processes. It also recognizes the historical and systemic injustices faced by these communities and the need for fair and equitable treatment in environmental policies and regulations.



### Subsection: 13.1b Role of Environmental Justice in Brownfield Redevelopment



Environmental justice plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment by addressing the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. Brownfields, which are abandoned or underutilized properties with potential environmental contamination, are often located in low-income and minority neighborhoods. These communities are already facing numerous social and economic challenges, and the presence of brownfields only exacerbates their struggles.



One of the key roles of environmental justice in brownfield redevelopment is to ensure that these communities are not further burdened by the negative impacts of contaminated sites. This includes addressing potential health risks, such as exposure to toxic chemicals, as well as economic and social impacts, such as decreased property values and limited access to resources and amenities. By involving these communities in the decision-making process and considering their needs and concerns, environmental justice can help mitigate these negative impacts and promote equitable redevelopment.



Furthermore, environmental justice also plays a role in promoting sustainable and equitable redevelopment practices. This includes incorporating green infrastructure, promoting energy efficiency, and creating affordable housing options in brownfield redevelopment projects. These practices not only benefit the environment but also improve the quality of life for residents in these communities. Environmental justice also advocates for the inclusion of local residents in the job opportunities created by brownfield redevelopment, providing economic benefits to the community.



In addition to addressing the impacts of brownfield redevelopment on marginalized communities, environmental justice also advocates for the inclusion of these communities in the benefits of redevelopment. This includes ensuring that residents have access to affordable housing, green spaces, and other amenities that are often lacking in these neighborhoods. By promoting equitable distribution of benefits, environmental justice can help create more sustainable and thriving communities.



Overall, environmental justice plays a critical role in brownfield redevelopment by addressing the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities and promoting equitable and sustainable redevelopment practices. By involving these communities in the decision-making process and advocating for their needs and concerns, environmental justice can help create more just and inclusive brownfield redevelopment projects.





### Section: 13.1 Environmental Justice and Brownfields:



Environmental justice is a critical aspect of brownfield redevelopment, as it seeks to address the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. These communities, often low-income and minority populations, are more likely to live near contaminated sites and suffer from the negative health and environmental impacts. Therefore, it is essential to define environmental justice and understand its role in brownfield redevelopment.



#### 13.1a Definition of Environmental Justice



Environmental justice is a social movement that aims to address environmental injustice, which is the exposure of poor and marginalized communities to harm from hazardous waste, resource extraction, and other land uses from which they do not receive benefits. It is a concept that encompasses distributive, procedural, and recognition justice. Distributive justice refers to the equitable distribution of environmental risks and benefits, while procedural justice focuses on the fair and meaningful participation of all stakeholders in decision-making processes. Recognition justice recognizes the oppression and difference in environmental justice communities and seeks to address them. Additionally, the capacity of these communities to convert social goods into a flourishing society is also a crucial aspect of environmental justice.



The United States Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) defines environmental justice as "the fair treatment and meaningful involvement of all people regardless of race, color, national origin, or income, with respect to the development, implementation, and enforcement of environmental laws, regulations, and policies." This definition highlights the importance of including all stakeholders, especially those from marginalized communities, in environmental decision-making processes. It also recognizes the historical and systemic injustices faced by these communities and the need for fair and equitable treatment in environmental policies and regulations.



#### 13.1b Environmental Justice and Brownfield Redevelopment



Brownfield redevelopment, which involves the revitalization of contaminated or abandoned sites, can have significant impacts on environmental justice communities. These communities are often located near brownfield sites due to historical patterns of segregation and discrimination. As a result, they bear a disproportionate burden of environmental hazards and lack access to environmental benefits such as parks and green spaces.



The redevelopment of brownfield sites can either exacerbate or alleviate environmental justice issues. On one hand, if not done carefully, it can lead to gentrification and displacement of low-income and minority communities. This can further perpetuate environmental injustice by pushing these communities to other polluted areas. On the other hand, brownfield redevelopment can also bring economic and environmental benefits to these communities if done in a socially and environmentally responsible manner.



#### 13.1c Case Studies in Environmental Justice and Brownfield Redevelopment



There have been numerous case studies that highlight the intersection of environmental justice and brownfield redevelopment. One such example is the South Bronx in New York City, which has a long history of environmental injustice. The area is home to numerous brownfield sites and has high rates of pollution and health issues among its predominantly low-income and minority population. However, through community-led efforts and partnerships with government agencies and private developers, the South Bronx has seen successful brownfield redevelopment projects that have brought economic opportunities and improved environmental conditions for its residents.



Another case study is the City of Richmond in California, which has a large population of low-income and minority communities living near industrial sites and oil refineries. The city has faced numerous environmental justice issues, including high levels of pollution and health problems. However, through community organizing and collaboration with local government and businesses, the city has successfully revitalized several brownfield sites, creating new parks and green spaces for its residents.



These case studies demonstrate the importance of considering environmental justice in brownfield redevelopment projects. By involving and empowering marginalized communities in the decision-making process, brownfield redevelopment can not only revitalize contaminated sites but also address environmental injustice and promote social and economic equity. 





### Section: 13.2 Environmental Justice Strategies:



Environmental justice strategies are essential for addressing the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. These strategies aim to promote equitable and sustainable development, as well as meaningful community involvement in decision-making processes. In this section, we will provide an overview of some key environmental justice strategies that can be applied to brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 13.2a Overview of Environmental Justice Strategies



There are various environmental justice strategies that can be utilized in brownfield redevelopment projects. These strategies can be broadly categorized into three types: prevention, mitigation, and remediation.



Prevention strategies focus on preventing environmental hazards from occurring in the first place. This can include measures such as zoning regulations to prevent the location of hazardous facilities in marginalized communities, as well as promoting sustainable and environmentally-friendly development practices.



Mitigation strategies aim to reduce the negative impacts of existing environmental hazards on communities. This can include measures such as implementing pollution control technologies and promoting green infrastructure to improve air and water quality in affected areas.



Remediation strategies involve the cleanup and restoration of contaminated sites. This can include measures such as soil remediation and groundwater treatment to remove pollutants and restore the site to a safe and healthy condition.



In addition to these strategies, community involvement and empowerment are crucial components of environmental justice. This can include providing opportunities for community members to participate in decision-making processes and ensuring that their voices are heard and considered in the planning and implementation of brownfield redevelopment projects.



Furthermore, it is essential to address the underlying social and economic inequalities that contribute to environmental injustice. This can include providing job training and employment opportunities for community members, as well as promoting affordable housing and transportation options in the redevelopment of brownfield sites.



Overall, a combination of prevention, mitigation, and remediation strategies, along with community involvement and addressing social and economic inequalities, can help promote environmental justice in brownfield redevelopment projects. It is crucial for environmental planners and practitioners to consider these strategies and work towards creating equitable and sustainable communities for all.





### Section: 13.2 Environmental Justice Strategies:



Environmental justice strategies are crucial for addressing the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. These strategies aim to promote equitable and sustainable development, as well as meaningful community involvement in decision-making processes. In this section, we will provide an overview of some key environmental justice strategies that can be applied to brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 13.2a Overview of Environmental Justice Strategies



There are various environmental justice strategies that can be utilized in brownfield redevelopment projects. These strategies can be broadly categorized into three types: prevention, mitigation, and remediation.



Prevention strategies focus on preventing environmental hazards from occurring in the first place. This can include measures such as zoning regulations to prevent the location of hazardous facilities in marginalized communities, as well as promoting sustainable and environmentally-friendly development practices. For example, implementing green building practices and promoting renewable energy sources can help prevent the creation of new environmental hazards in already overburdened communities.



Mitigation strategies aim to reduce the negative impacts of existing environmental hazards on communities. This can include measures such as implementing pollution control technologies and promoting green infrastructure to improve air and water quality in affected areas. For instance, installing air filtration systems in schools and community centers located near industrial sites can help mitigate the health risks associated with air pollution.



Remediation strategies involve the cleanup and restoration of contaminated sites. This can include measures such as soil remediation and groundwater treatment to remove pollutants and restore the site to a safe and healthy condition. In addition to cleaning up the physical environment, remediation strategies should also address the social and economic impacts of environmental hazards on communities. This can include providing job training and employment opportunities for community members in the cleanup and redevelopment process.



In addition to these strategies, community involvement and empowerment are crucial components of environmental justice. This can include providing opportunities for community members to participate in decision-making processes and ensuring that their voices are heard and considered in the planning and implementation of brownfield redevelopment projects. Community involvement can also help identify and prioritize the specific needs and concerns of the affected community, leading to more effective and sustainable solutions.



Furthermore, it is essential to address the underlying social and economic factors that contribute to environmental injustice. This can include addressing systemic racism and economic disparities that often result in marginalized communities bearing a disproportionate burden of environmental hazards. Environmental justice strategies should also prioritize the protection and promotion of the rights and well-being of vulnerable populations, including low-income communities, communities of color, and indigenous communities.



In conclusion, a comprehensive approach to environmental justice in brownfield redevelopment projects should include a combination of prevention, mitigation, and remediation strategies, as well as meaningful community involvement and addressing underlying social and economic factors. By implementing these strategies, we can work towards creating more equitable and sustainable communities for all.





### Section: 13.2 Environmental Justice Strategies:



Environmental justice strategies are crucial for addressing the disproportionate burden of environmental hazards on marginalized communities. These strategies aim to promote equitable and sustainable development, as well as meaningful community involvement in decision-making processes. In this section, we will provide an overview of some key environmental justice strategies that can be applied to brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 13.2a Overview of Environmental Justice Strategies



There are various environmental justice strategies that can be utilized in brownfield redevelopment projects. These strategies can be broadly categorized into three types: prevention, mitigation, and remediation.



Prevention strategies focus on preventing environmental hazards from occurring in the first place. This can include measures such as zoning regulations to prevent the location of hazardous facilities in marginalized communities, as well as promoting sustainable and environmentally-friendly development practices. For example, implementing green building practices and promoting renewable energy sources can help prevent the creation of new environmental hazards in already overburdened communities.



Mitigation strategies aim to reduce the negative impacts of existing environmental hazards on communities. This can include measures such as implementing pollution control technologies and promoting green infrastructure to improve air and water quality in affected areas. For instance, installing air filtration systems in schools and community centers located near industrial sites can help mitigate the health risks associated with air pollution.



Remediation strategies involve the cleanup and restoration of contaminated sites. This can include measures such as soil remediation and groundwater treatment to remove pollutants and restore the site to a safe and healthy condition. In addition to cleaning up the physical environment, remediation strategies should also address the social and economic impacts of the contamination on the affected community. This can include providing job training and employment opportunities for community members in the cleanup and redevelopment process.



#### 13.2b Environmental Justice and Community Engagement



One key aspect of environmental justice strategies is community engagement. It is important for community members to be involved in decision-making processes and have a say in how their environment is managed. This can help ensure that their needs and concerns are taken into account and that the solutions implemented are culturally and socially appropriate.



Community engagement can take various forms, such as public meetings, focus groups, and participatory planning processes. It is important for these processes to be inclusive and accessible to all members of the community, including those who may not have traditionally been involved in environmental decision-making. This can help promote social equity and empower marginalized communities to have a voice in shaping their environment.



#### 13.2c Case Studies in Environmental Justice Strategies



To further illustrate the effectiveness of environmental justice strategies in brownfield redevelopment, let's take a look at some case studies from different countries.



In Argentina, the environmental justice movement has been successful in advocating for the relocation of hazardous waste facilities away from marginalized communities. In the city of La Matanza, community members organized protests and legal action to demand the closure of a waste incinerator that was causing health issues in the surrounding area. As a result, the incinerator was shut down and the community was able to reclaim their environment.



In Brazil, the environmental justice movement has focused on promoting sustainable development and renewable energy sources in marginalized communities. In the city of Porto Alegre, community members worked with local government and NGOs to install solar panels in low-income neighborhoods, providing clean and affordable energy to residents.



In Ecuador, the indigenous community of Sarayaku successfully fought against oil drilling on their land, which would have caused significant environmental and cultural damage. Through protests, legal action, and international advocacy, the community was able to protect their land and preserve their way of life.



In Peru, the environmental justice movement has focused on remediation strategies for contaminated sites. In the city of La Oroya, community members worked with government agencies and NGOs to clean up a lead smelter that had been polluting the air and water for decades. The cleanup not only improved the health of the community, but also created job opportunities for local residents.



These case studies demonstrate the importance and effectiveness of environmental justice strategies in addressing environmental issues in marginalized communities. By involving community members in decision-making processes and implementing prevention, mitigation, and remediation strategies, we can work towards a more equitable and sustainable future for all.





### Section: 13.3 Environmental Justice and Community Engagement:



Environmental justice is a critical aspect of environmental planning and management, and it is essential to consider the role of community engagement in achieving environmental justice. Community engagement refers to the active involvement of community members in decision-making processes that affect their environment and well-being. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement is crucial for addressing environmental justice issues and promoting equitable and sustainable development.



#### 13.3a Role of Community Engagement in Environmental Justice



Community engagement plays a vital role in environmental justice by empowering marginalized communities to participate in decision-making processes that affect their environment. It allows community members to voice their concerns and advocate for their rights, ensuring that their needs and perspectives are considered in the decision-making process. This is especially important in the context of brownfield redevelopment, where communities may have been disproportionately impacted by environmental hazards and may have a vested interest in the future use of the site.



One of the key benefits of community engagement in environmental justice is the promotion of equity and fairness. By involving community members in decision-making processes, it helps to ensure that the burden of environmental hazards is not unfairly placed on marginalized communities. It also allows for the identification and mitigation of potential environmental justice issues before they arise, promoting more equitable outcomes for all stakeholders.



Community engagement also plays a crucial role in promoting sustainable development. By involving community members in the decision-making process, it allows for the integration of local knowledge and perspectives, which can lead to more sustainable and culturally appropriate solutions. This is particularly important in the context of brownfield redevelopment, where the site may have historical or cultural significance to the community.



Furthermore, community engagement can help to build trust and foster positive relationships between community members and decision-makers. This can lead to more effective and collaborative decision-making processes, as well as increased accountability and transparency. By involving community members in the planning and implementation of brownfield redevelopment projects, it can also help to build a sense of ownership and pride in the community.



In the United States, there have been numerous examples of successful community engagement in brownfield redevelopment projects. For instance, the Environmental Protection Agency's Brownfields Program encourages community involvement in the assessment and cleanup of brownfield sites. This program provides funding and technical assistance to communities to facilitate their participation in the redevelopment process.



In addition to government initiatives, there are also various community-based organizations and grassroots movements that advocate for environmental justice and community engagement in brownfield redevelopment. These groups often act as watchdogs, monitoring and advocating for the rights of marginalized communities and promoting sustainable and equitable development practices.



In conclusion, community engagement is a crucial component of achieving environmental justice in brownfield redevelopment. It empowers marginalized communities to participate in decision-making processes, promotes equity and fairness, and fosters sustainable development. By involving community members in the planning and implementation of brownfield redevelopment projects, we can ensure that the needs and perspectives of all stakeholders are considered, leading to more equitable and sustainable outcomes.

### Subsection: 13.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Environmental Justice



There are various techniques and strategies that can be used to engage communities in environmental justice. These techniques can be broadly categorized into three main approaches: information sharing, consultation, and collaboration.



#### Information Sharing



Information sharing involves providing community members with relevant information about the environmental issues and decision-making processes that affect their community. This can include sharing data on environmental hazards, potential health risks, and proposed solutions. Information sharing is an essential first step in engaging communities, as it allows community members to understand the issues at hand and make informed decisions.



#### Consultation



Consultation involves seeking input and feedback from community members on proposed solutions or decisions. This can take various forms, such as public meetings, surveys, or focus groups. Consultation allows for community members to voice their concerns and provide valuable insights that can inform decision-making. It also helps to build trust and foster a sense of ownership among community members.



#### Collaboration



Collaboration involves working together with community members to develop solutions and make decisions. This approach recognizes the expertise and knowledge of community members and involves them as equal partners in the decision-making process. Collaboration can take various forms, such as community-based participatory research, co-design processes, or community-led initiatives. This approach can lead to more sustainable and culturally appropriate solutions that address the needs and priorities of the community.



In addition to these approaches, it is essential to consider the cultural and linguistic diversity of the community when engaging in environmental justice. This may require the use of interpreters, translated materials, and culturally sensitive communication strategies to ensure that all community members can participate fully.



Overall, community engagement is a crucial aspect of achieving environmental justice in brownfield redevelopment. By involving community members in decision-making processes, it promotes equity, fairness, and sustainable development. It also helps to build trust and foster a sense of ownership among community members, leading to more successful and inclusive outcomes. 





### Section: 13.3 Environmental Justice and Community Engagement:



Environmental justice is a critical aspect of environmental planning and management, and it is essential to consider the role of community engagement in achieving environmental justice. Community engagement refers to the active involvement of community members in decision-making processes that affect their environment and well-being. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement is crucial for addressing environmental justice issues and promoting equitable and sustainable development.



#### 13.3a Role of Community Engagement in Environmental Justice

This is particularly important in the context of brownfield redevelopment, where the history and culture of the community must be taken into account to ensure a successful and sustainable redevelopment project.



### 13.3b Community Engagement Strategies for Environmental Justice



There are various strategies that can be employed to engage communities in the decision-making process for environmental justice. One approach is community-based participatory research (CBPR), which involves collaboration between community members and researchers to address community concerns and promote social change. This approach has been successfully used by organizations such as Communities for a Better Environment (CBE) to advocate for policy change and address environmental justice issues.



Another strategy is the use of community meetings and forums, where community members can voice their concerns and provide input on decision-making processes. These meetings can also serve as a platform for education and empowerment, allowing community members to learn about their rights and how to advocate for their needs.



In addition, political education and advocacy can also be effective in engaging communities in environmental justice. This involves educating community members on the political processes and policies that affect their environment and empowering them to advocate for change. School groups and youth organizations can also play a crucial role in educating and mobilizing the next generation to be active participants in environmental justice.



### 13.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Environmental Justice



To further illustrate the importance and effectiveness of community engagement in environmental justice, let us examine two case studies.



1. Community-Based Participatory Research as a Tool for Policy Change: A Case Study of the Southern California Environmental Justice Collaborative.



In this case study, CBE collaborated with the Liberty Hill Foundation and an academic research team to advocate for policy change in the South Coast Air Quality Management District. Through their collaboration, they were able to successfully reduce the maximum individual cancer risk from 100 to 25 risks per million, representing a significant reduction in acceptable risk levels. This collaboration not only led to tangible policy change but also raised awareness and empowered the community to advocate for their rights.



2. Linking Exposure Assessment Science With Policy: A Case Study of the Love Canal Community.



The Love Canal community in Niagara Falls, New York, is a prime example of the devastating effects of environmental injustice. The community was built on top of a toxic waste dump, and residents were exposed to hazardous chemicals for decades. Through community engagement and advocacy, the residents were able to bring attention to their situation and successfully relocate to a safer area. This case study highlights the importance of community engagement in addressing environmental justice issues and promoting the well-being of marginalized communities.



In conclusion, community engagement is a crucial aspect of achieving environmental justice in the context of brownfield redevelopment. It empowers marginalized communities to participate in decision-making processes, promotes equity and fairness, and leads to more sustainable and culturally appropriate solutions. Through strategies such as community-based participatory research, community meetings, and political education, communities can effectively advocate for their rights and create positive change in their environment. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and environmental justice. We have seen how the redevelopment of contaminated sites can have both positive and negative impacts on marginalized communities. We have also discussed the importance of incorporating environmental justice principles into the planning and implementation of brownfield redevelopment projects.



One key takeaway from this chapter is the need for community involvement and engagement in the brownfield redevelopment process. By involving community members in decision-making and planning, we can ensure that their voices and concerns are heard and addressed. This can help to mitigate potential negative impacts and promote more equitable outcomes.



Another important consideration is the use of environmental justice screening tools to identify potential environmental justice concerns in the early stages of a brownfield redevelopment project. These tools can help to guide decision-making and ensure that environmental justice is prioritized throughout the process.



Overall, it is clear that brownfield redevelopment and environmental justice are closely intertwined. As environmental practitioners, it is our responsibility to ensure that these two concepts are integrated into our work to promote sustainable and equitable outcomes for all communities.

#### Exercise 2: Environmental Justice Screening

Research and identify an environmental justice screening tool that could be used in a brownfield redevelopment project. How does this tool work and what information does it provide? How could it be used to inform decision-making and promote environmental justice?

How were environmental justice concerns addressed and what lessons can be learned from this project?

Create a stakeholder map for a hypothetical brownfield redevelopment project. Identify all relevant stakeholders, including community members, government agencies, and private developers. How could these stakeholders work together to promote environmental justice in the project?

## Chapter: Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment



### Introduction:



In this chapter, we will be discussing the topic of brownfield redevelopment and its relationship with green infrastructure. Brownfield redevelopment refers to the process of revitalizing and reusing abandoned or underutilized properties that may be contaminated with hazardous substances. These properties, known as brownfields, can be found in both urban and rural areas and are often a result of past industrial or commercial activities.



The redevelopment of brownfields presents a unique set of challenges and opportunities for environmental management. On one hand, it provides a chance to revitalize and repurpose land that may have been neglected or abandoned for years. On the other hand, it also requires careful consideration and management of potential environmental hazards that may be present on the site.



In recent years, there has been a growing emphasis on incorporating green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment projects. Green infrastructure refers to the use of natural systems and processes to manage stormwater and provide other environmental benefits. This approach not only helps to mitigate potential environmental impacts of redevelopment, but also provides additional benefits such as improved air and water quality, increased biodiversity, and enhanced community resilience.



Throughout this chapter, we will explore the various aspects of brownfield redevelopment and its relationship with green infrastructure. We will discuss the challenges and opportunities of redeveloping brownfields, as well as the benefits and considerations of incorporating green infrastructure into these projects. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of the complexities and potential solutions involved in brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure.





### Section: 14.1 Green Infrastructure and Brownfields:



Brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure are two important concepts in environmental management. In this section, we will define green infrastructure and discuss its relationship with brownfield redevelopment.



#### 14.1a Definition of Green Infrastructure



Green infrastructure can be defined as a network of natural systems and processes that are used to manage stormwater and provide other environmental benefits. This approach utilizes plants, soil, and other natural elements to mimic the functions of natural ecosystems and provide a range of services, such as stormwater management, climate adaptation, and biodiversity conservation.



Green infrastructure is a subset of sustainable and resilient infrastructure, which is defined in standards such as SuRe, the Standard for Sustainable and Resilient Infrastructure. However, it also encompasses low-carbon infrastructure, such as renewable energy infrastructure and public transportation systems. Additionally, green infrastructure can be a component of sustainable drainage systems (SuDS) or sustainable urban drainage systems (SUDS), which are designed to manage water quantity and quality while also providing improvements to biodiversity and amenity.



Green infrastructure is an important tool in environmental management as it helps to mitigate the impacts of urbanization and climate change. By incorporating natural systems into urban areas, green infrastructure can help to reduce stormwater runoff, improve air and water quality, and increase biodiversity. It also has the potential to enhance community resilience by providing shade and shelter, promoting social inclusion, and creating opportunities for recreation and food production.



#### 14.1b Relationship with Brownfield Redevelopment



Brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure have a symbiotic relationship. Brownfield redevelopment projects often involve the remediation and reuse of contaminated land, which can have significant environmental impacts. By incorporating green infrastructure into these projects, the potential environmental impacts can be mitigated, and additional benefits can be realized.



For example, green infrastructure can be used to manage stormwater runoff from brownfield sites, reducing the risk of contamination to surrounding areas. It can also help to improve air and water quality, which may have been impacted by previous industrial activities. Additionally, green infrastructure can enhance the aesthetic value of brownfield sites, making them more attractive for redevelopment and improving the quality of life for surrounding communities.



On the other hand, brownfield redevelopment can also provide opportunities for the implementation of green infrastructure. As contaminated land is often abandoned or underutilized, it provides a blank canvas for the incorporation of natural systems. This can be especially beneficial in urban areas where green space is limited. By incorporating green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment projects, cities can increase their resilience to climate change and improve the overall health and well-being of their residents.



In conclusion, green infrastructure and brownfield redevelopment are closely intertwined and can provide numerous benefits when implemented together. By utilizing natural systems in the redevelopment of contaminated land, we can create more sustainable and resilient communities while also mitigating the potential environmental impacts of urbanization and climate change. 





### Section: 14.1 Green Infrastructure and Brownfields:



Brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure are two important concepts in environmental management. In this section, we will define green infrastructure and discuss its role in brownfield redevelopment.



#### 14.1a Definition of Green Infrastructure



Green infrastructure can be defined as a network of natural systems and processes that are used to manage stormwater and provide other environmental benefits. This approach utilizes plants, soil, and other natural elements to mimic the functions of natural ecosystems and provide a range of services, such as stormwater management, climate adaptation, and biodiversity conservation.



Green infrastructure is a subset of sustainable and resilient infrastructure, which is defined in standards such as SuRe, the Standard for Sustainable and Resilient Infrastructure. However, it also encompasses low-carbon infrastructure, such as renewable energy infrastructure and public transportation systems. Additionally, green infrastructure can be a component of sustainable drainage systems (SuDS) or sustainable urban drainage systems (SUDS), which are designed to manage water quantity and quality while also providing improvements to biodiversity and amenity.



Green infrastructure is an important tool in environmental management as it helps to mitigate the impacts of urbanization and climate change. By incorporating natural systems into urban areas, green infrastructure can help to reduce stormwater runoff, improve air and water quality, and increase biodiversity. It also has the potential to enhance community resilience by providing shade and shelter, promoting social inclusion, and creating opportunities for recreation and food production.



#### 14.1b Role of Green Infrastructure in Brownfield Redevelopment



Green infrastructure plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment projects. Brownfields are abandoned or underutilized properties that may be contaminated with hazardous substances. These sites often pose environmental and health risks to nearby communities and can be a barrier to economic development. However, with the use of green infrastructure, these sites can be transformed into sustainable and resilient spaces that benefit both the environment and the community.



One way green infrastructure can be incorporated into brownfield redevelopment is through the use of green roofs. Green roofs, which are partially or completely covered with vegetation, can help to reduce stormwater runoff and improve air quality. They also provide insulation, reducing energy usage and carbon emissions. By implementing green roofs on brownfield sites, developers can mitigate the environmental impacts of the site while also creating a more sustainable and attractive space for future use.



Another way green infrastructure can be utilized in brownfield redevelopment is through tree planting. Trees provide a host of ecological, social, and economic benefits, making them an essential component of green infrastructure. In brownfield sites, trees can help to improve air and water quality, reduce the urban heat island effect, and provide shade and shelter for the community. They also have the potential to increase property values and attract businesses and residents to the area.



In addition to these benefits, green infrastructure can also help to reduce the costs of brownfield redevelopment. By incorporating natural systems into the site, developers can save on the costs of traditional stormwater management infrastructure and potentially receive tax incentives for implementing green infrastructure practices.



In conclusion, green infrastructure plays a vital role in brownfield redevelopment. By utilizing natural systems and processes, brownfield sites can be transformed into sustainable and resilient spaces that benefit both the environment and the community. As the demand for sustainable development continues to grow, the incorporation of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment will become increasingly important. 





### Section: 14.1 Green Infrastructure and Brownfields:



Green infrastructure and brownfield redevelopment are two important concepts in environmental management. In this section, we will discuss the role of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment through case studies.



#### 14.1c Case Studies in Green Infrastructure and Brownfield Redevelopment



Green infrastructure has been successfully implemented in various brownfield redevelopment projects, providing numerous environmental and social benefits. Let's take a look at some case studies that showcase the effectiveness of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment.



##### Case Study 1: The High Line, New York City



The High Line is a 1.45-mile-long elevated linear park built on a former freight rail line in Manhattan, New York City. The abandoned railway was transformed into a green space with walking paths, gardens, and seating areas. The project not only revitalized the area but also provided numerous environmental benefits. The green space helps to mitigate the urban heat island effect, reduce stormwater runoff, and improve air quality. It also serves as a habitat for various plant and animal species, increasing biodiversity in the area.



##### Case Study 2: The Steel Yard, Providence



The Steel Yard is a former industrial site in Providence, Rhode Island that has been transformed into a community arts and industrial space. The project incorporated green infrastructure elements such as rain gardens, bioswales, and permeable pavement to manage stormwater runoff from the site. These features not only reduce the burden on the city's stormwater system but also improve the water quality of the nearby Woonasquatucket River. The Steel Yard also provides educational opportunities for the community on sustainable practices and serves as a hub for local artists and small businesses.



##### Case Study 3: The Brewery District, Toronto



The Brewery District in Toronto, Canada is a former industrial site that has been redeveloped into a mixed-use community with residential, commercial, and recreational spaces. The project incorporated green infrastructure elements such as green roofs, rain gardens, and permeable pavement to manage stormwater runoff and reduce the heat island effect. These features also provide numerous social benefits, such as creating a more livable and walkable community and promoting social interaction among residents.



These case studies demonstrate the effectiveness of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment. By incorporating natural systems into urban areas, green infrastructure not only provides environmental benefits but also enhances the livability and resilience of communities. It is a crucial tool in promoting sustainable and resilient development in our cities.





### Section: 14.2 Green Infrastructure Strategies:



Green infrastructure strategies are becoming increasingly popular in brownfield redevelopment projects due to their effectiveness in providing ecological, economic, and social benefits. These strategies utilize natural systems and processes to manage water and create healthier urban environments. In this section, we will provide an overview of some common green infrastructure strategies and their benefits.



#### 14.2a Overview of Green Infrastructure Strategies



Green roofs, tree planting, and permeable pavement are some of the most commonly used green infrastructure strategies. These strategies can be implemented individually or in combination to achieve maximum benefits.



##### Green Roofs



A green roof is a rooftop that is partially or completely covered with vegetation. It includes additional layers such as a root barrier, drainage, and irrigation systems. Green roofs can be categorized as extensive (with a growing media depth ranging from two to six inches) or intensive (with a growing media depth greater than six inches).



Green roofs provide numerous benefits, including reducing stormwater runoff by storing water in the growing media. This reduces the burden on the sewer system and waterways, decreasing the risk of combined sewer overflows. Additionally, green roofs reduce energy usage by providing insulation, reducing solar radiation on the roof's surface, and providing evaporative cooling. This helps to lower roof surface temperatures and heat influx, ultimately reducing energy consumption and carbon dioxide emissions from electricity generation.



##### Tree Planting



Tree planting is another commonly used green infrastructure strategy that provides a host of ecological, social, and economic benefits. Trees help to mitigate the urban heat island effect by providing shade and reducing surface temperatures. They also improve air quality by absorbing pollutants and producing oxygen. Trees also provide habitat for various plant and animal species, increasing biodiversity in urban areas.



In addition to these environmental benefits, tree planting also has social and economic benefits. Trees can improve the aesthetics of an area, increase property values, and provide recreational opportunities for the community.



##### Permeable Pavement



Permeable pavement is a type of pavement that allows water to pass through it and into the ground below. This helps to reduce stormwater runoff and replenish groundwater supplies. Permeable pavement can be made from a variety of materials, including porous concrete, permeable asphalt, and permeable pavers.



In addition to managing stormwater, permeable pavement also helps to reduce the urban heat island effect by allowing water to evaporate from the pavement's surface. It also reduces the need for traditional stormwater infrastructure, saving cities money in maintenance and construction costs.



In conclusion, green infrastructure strategies such as green roofs, tree planting, and permeable pavement provide numerous benefits in brownfield redevelopment projects. These strategies not only help to manage stormwater and improve air quality but also provide social and economic benefits for the community. As such, they are becoming increasingly popular in environmental management practicum projects.





### Section: 14.2 Green Infrastructure Strategies:



Green infrastructure strategies are becoming increasingly popular in brownfield redevelopment projects due to their effectiveness in providing ecological, economic, and social benefits. These strategies utilize natural systems and processes to manage water and create healthier urban environments.
