This can be costly and time-consuming, and may also require specialized expertise. Additionally, there may be concerns about the safety and environmental impact of reusing a structure that may have been contaminated by hazardous substances.



### Subsection: 16.1b Role of Adaptive Reuse in Brownfield Redevelopment



Adaptive reuse plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment by providing a sustainable and cost-effective solution for repurposing abandoned or underutilized properties. By reusing existing structures, adaptive reuse reduces the need for new construction and the associated environmental impacts, such as the depletion of natural resources and the generation of construction waste.



Moreover, adaptive reuse can also help preserve the historical and cultural significance of a community by repurposing old buildings and structures. This not only adds character to the community but also promotes a sense of identity and pride among its residents.



In the context of brownfield redevelopment, adaptive reuse also helps mitigate the potential risks associated with contaminated sites. By repurposing existing structures, the need for extensive excavation and soil remediation is reduced, minimizing the potential for exposure to hazardous substances. This is particularly beneficial for communities living near brownfield sites, as it helps mitigate the potential health risks associated with exposure to pollutants.



Furthermore, adaptive reuse can also bring economic benefits to a community. By repurposing existing structures, the cost of new construction is reduced, making it a more financially viable option for smaller communities or organizations with limited resources. Additionally, adaptive reuse can also create new economic opportunities, such as job creation and increased property values, which can contribute to the overall revitalization of a community.



In conclusion, adaptive reuse plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment by providing a sustainable, cost-effective, and community-oriented approach to repurposing abandoned or underutilized properties. By preserving the historical and cultural significance of a community, mitigating potential risks associated with contaminated sites, and bringing economic benefits, adaptive reuse is an essential strategy for promoting sustainable land use and revitalizing communities.





### Section: 16.1 Adaptive Reuse and Brownfields:



Adaptive reuse and brownfield redevelopment are two important strategies for addressing the issue of abandoned or underutilized properties that may have been contaminated by hazardous substances. These properties, known as brownfields, pose a threat to public health and the environment, and their redevelopment is essential for revitalizing communities and promoting sustainable land use.



#### 16.1a Definition of Adaptive Reuse



Adaptive reuse is the process of repurposing existing structures for new uses. This concept is closely related to the idea of recycling, but instead of materials, it involves reusing buildings and other structures. Adaptive reuse is a sustainable practice that helps reduce waste and conserve resources, as well as preserve historical and cultural landmarks.



Adaptive reuse is particularly relevant in the context of brownfield redevelopment. Many brownfield sites contain old industrial buildings or other structures that are no longer in use. These structures often have historical or architectural value, and adaptive reuse allows them to be repurposed instead of being demolished. This not only preserves the character of the community but also reduces the environmental impact of redevelopment by avoiding the need for new construction.



One example of adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment is the conversion of an old factory into a mixed-use development with residential, commercial, and recreational spaces. This not only brings new life to the abandoned property but also provides much-needed housing and economic opportunities for the community.



Adaptive reuse also has economic benefits. It is often more cost-effective than new construction, as the existing structure can be renovated and repurposed instead of starting from scratch. This can be especially beneficial for smaller communities or organizations with limited resources.



However, adaptive reuse also has its challenges. The existing structure may not be suitable for the intended new use, requiring significant renovations and upgrades. This can be costly and time-consuming. Additionally, there may be concerns about the potential presence of hazardous substances in the building, which could pose health risks to occupants. Proper remediation and management of these substances is crucial in ensuring the safety of the new occupants and the surrounding environment.



### 16.1b Definition of Brownfield Redevelopment



Brownfield redevelopment is the process of revitalizing and redeveloping abandoned or underutilized properties that may have been contaminated by hazardous substances. These properties, known as brownfields, are often located in urban areas and were previously used for industrial or commercial purposes. The Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) defines a brownfield as "a property, the expansion, redevelopment, or reuse of which may be complicated by the presence or potential presence of a hazardous substance, pollutant, or contaminant."



The redevelopment of brownfields is crucial for promoting sustainable land use and revitalizing communities. These properties often have a negative impact on the surrounding environment and can pose health risks to nearby residents. By redeveloping these properties, we can mitigate these risks and create new opportunities for economic growth and community development.



### 16.1c Case Studies in Adaptive Reuse and Brownfield Redevelopment



To better understand the concepts of adaptive reuse and brownfield redevelopment, let's take a look at some real-world case studies.



One successful example of adaptive reuse and brownfield redevelopment is the High Line in New York City. The High Line is a 1.45-mile-long elevated park built on an abandoned freight rail line on Manhattan's West Side. The rail line had been out of use since 1980 and was at risk of being demolished. However, a community-led effort to repurpose the rail line into a public park was successful, and the High Line opened in 2009. The project not only preserved a piece of the city's history but also created a new public space for residents and visitors to enjoy.



Another notable case study is the redevelopment of the former Bethlehem Steel plant in Bethlehem, Pennsylvania. The plant, which had been in operation for over 140 years, closed in 1995, leaving behind a massive brownfield site. The site was contaminated with hazardous substances, including heavy metals and petroleum products. However, through a partnership between the city, state, and private developers, the site was successfully remediated and redeveloped into a mixed-use community with residential, commercial, and recreational spaces. The project has brought new life to the area and has been recognized as a model for brownfield redevelopment.



These case studies demonstrate the potential for adaptive reuse and brownfield redevelopment to transform abandoned and contaminated properties into vibrant and sustainable spaces. However, it is important to note that each project is unique and requires careful planning, collaboration, and remediation to ensure the safety and success of the redevelopment. 





### Section: 16.2 Adaptive Reuse Strategies:



Adaptive reuse is a valuable strategy for addressing the issue of abandoned or underutilized properties, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. In this section, we will discuss some key strategies for implementing adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 16.2a Overview of Adaptive Reuse Strategies



Adaptive reuse strategies can be broadly categorized into three main approaches: preservation, rehabilitation, and renovation. Each approach involves different levels of intervention and can be applied to different types of structures.



Preservation involves minimal intervention and focuses on maintaining the original character and features of a structure. This approach is often used for historically significant buildings or structures with unique architectural features. Preservation may involve minor repairs and maintenance to ensure the structure remains stable and safe, but the goal is to preserve the original structure as much as possible.



Rehabilitation involves more significant interventions, such as repairing or replacing damaged or deteriorated elements of a structure. This approach is often used for structures that have some historical or architectural value but may not be considered significant enough for preservation. Rehabilitation may also involve updating the structure to meet current building codes and standards.



Renovation involves the most extensive interventions and often results in a complete transformation of the structure. This approach is typically used for structures that have little historical or architectural value but can be repurposed for a new use. Renovation may involve significant modifications to the structure, such as adding new floors or changing the layout, to accommodate the new use.



When considering which approach to use for a specific structure, it is important to consider the goals of the project, the condition of the structure, and the resources available. Preservation may be the best option for structures with significant historical or architectural value, while renovation may be more suitable for structures that can be completely transformed for a new use.



In addition to these three main approaches, there are also various techniques and methods that can be used in adaptive reuse projects. These include:



- Adaptive reuse of materials: This involves salvaging and repurposing materials from the existing structure, reducing waste and conserving resources.

- Adaptive reuse of space: This involves reconfiguring the layout of a structure to accommodate a new use, such as converting a warehouse into a residential building.

- Adaptive reuse of systems: This involves repurposing existing systems, such as plumbing or electrical, to meet the needs of the new use.

- Adaptive reuse of technology: This involves incorporating new technology into the existing structure, such as installing energy-efficient systems or using smart building technology.



By utilizing these strategies and techniques, adaptive reuse can be a sustainable and cost-effective approach to brownfield redevelopment. It not only helps revitalize communities and preserve historical and cultural landmarks but also promotes responsible land use and resource conservation.





### Section: 16.2 Adaptive Reuse Strategies:



Adaptive reuse is a valuable strategy for addressing the issue of abandoned or underutilized properties, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. In this section, we will discuss some key strategies for implementing adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 16.2b Selection of Adaptive Reuse Strategies



When considering which adaptive reuse strategy to use for a specific structure, there are several factors that must be taken into account. These include the goals of the project, the condition of the structure, and the available resources.



The first step in selecting an adaptive reuse strategy is to clearly define the goals of the project. This may include preserving the historical or architectural significance of the structure, repurposing it for a new use, or simply ensuring its stability and safety. By clearly defining the goals, it becomes easier to determine which approach will best achieve those goals.



The condition of the structure is also an important factor to consider. Preservation may be the best approach for a structure with significant historical or architectural value, but if the structure is in poor condition, rehabilitation or renovation may be necessary to ensure its stability and safety. A thorough assessment of the structure's condition should be conducted before selecting an adaptive reuse strategy.



Another important consideration is the available resources. Preservation may be the ideal approach, but it can also be the most costly. If resources are limited, rehabilitation or renovation may be a more feasible option. It is important to carefully consider the budget and available resources before deciding on an adaptive reuse strategy.



In addition to these factors, it is also important to consult with experts in the field of adaptive reuse. Architects, engineers, and preservation specialists can provide valuable insights and recommendations for the best approach to take for a specific structure.



Ultimately, the selection of an adaptive reuse strategy should be a well-informed decision that takes into account the goals of the project, the condition of the structure, and the available resources. By carefully considering these factors and consulting with experts, the most appropriate and effective strategy can be chosen for each individual brownfield redevelopment project.





### Section: 16.2 Adaptive Reuse Strategies:



Adaptive reuse is a valuable strategy for addressing the issue of abandoned or underutilized properties, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. In this section, we will discuss some key strategies for implementing adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 16.2c Case Studies in Adaptive Reuse Strategies



To better understand the practical application of adaptive reuse strategies, let's take a look at some case studies of successful projects.



One notable example is the adaptive reuse of the IONA Technologies building in Dublin, Ireland. Originally built in the 1990s as a headquarters for the software company, the building was later abandoned and left vacant for several years. In 2016, the building was purchased by a developer who saw the potential for adaptive reuse. The building was renovated and repurposed into a mixed-use development, with office space, retail shops, and residential units. This project not only revitalized a previously abandoned property, but also brought new economic opportunities to the surrounding community.



Another successful case study is the adaptive reuse of a former industrial site in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania. The site, which was once home to a steel mill, had been abandoned for decades and was heavily contaminated with hazardous materials. Through a combination of remediation efforts and adaptive reuse strategies, the site was transformed into a mixed-use development with residential, commercial, and recreational spaces. This project not only cleaned up a contaminated brownfield, but also provided much-needed housing and job opportunities for the local community.



These case studies demonstrate the potential for adaptive reuse to not only revitalize abandoned properties, but also bring economic and social benefits to the surrounding community. However, it is important to note that the success of these projects was not without challenges. Factors such as budget constraints, structural condition, and community involvement all played a role in the decision-making process and implementation of adaptive reuse strategies.



In addition to these case studies, there are also several systematic approaches to implementing adaptive reuse strategies. These include the "app->comp" model, which allows for the reuse of existing applications as modules in new projects, and the CO paradigm, which emphasizes the optimization of reusability in data-oriented activities. By utilizing these systematic approaches, developers can further enhance the effectiveness and efficiency of adaptive reuse strategies.



In conclusion, adaptive reuse is a valuable tool in the redevelopment of brownfield sites. By carefully considering factors such as project goals, structural condition, and available resources, and by utilizing systematic approaches, developers can successfully implement adaptive reuse strategies and bring new life to abandoned properties. 





### Section: 16.3 Adaptive Reuse and Community Engagement:



Adaptive reuse is a valuable strategy for addressing the issue of abandoned or underutilized properties, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. However, the success of adaptive reuse projects is not solely dependent on the physical transformation of the property, but also on the engagement and involvement of the surrounding community. In this section, we will discuss the role of community engagement in adaptive reuse and its impact on the success of these projects.



#### 16.3a Role of Community Engagement in Adaptive Reuse



Community engagement plays a crucial role in the success of adaptive reuse projects. It involves actively involving the community in the decision-making process and incorporating their input and feedback into the project. This not only ensures that the project meets the needs and desires of the community, but also fosters a sense of ownership and pride in the project.



One way to engage the community in adaptive reuse projects is through open-source architecture. This approach recognizes the core role of multiple users at every stage of the project and harnesses the power of network effects to effectively scale systems. It also promotes principles of open access and participation, making it a democratic process. By involving the community in the design and planning process, open-source architecture allows for a more inclusive and collaborative approach to adaptive reuse.



Another important aspect of community engagement in adaptive reuse is the establishment of open standards of collaboration. This includes the use of common, open, modular standards that allow for collaborative efforts across networks. This not only promotes compatibility and efficiency in the project, but also encourages a sense of community and collaboration among stakeholders.



Engaging the community in adaptive reuse projects also helps to address potential challenges and concerns. Traditional engagement programs, such as focus groups, often result in lack of representation and input from the community. With crowd-funded models, forms of engagement are built into the process, enabling a kind of emergent urbanism where the use of space is optimized on terms set by its users. This reclamation of people's power can be seen as a soft, spatial version of Hacktivism.



However, it is important to note that community engagement in adaptive reuse projects may also face challenges. These include organizational drawbacks, such as forking of projects, abandoned projects, and the emergence of cliques. Additionally, organized campaigns of fear, uncertainty, and doubt may also arise. It is important for project leaders to address these challenges and ensure that the community remains engaged and informed throughout the project.



In conclusion, community engagement plays a crucial role in the success of adaptive reuse projects. By actively involving the community in the decision-making process and incorporating their input and feedback, adaptive reuse projects can not only revitalize abandoned properties, but also bring economic and social benefits to the surrounding community. 





### Section: 16.3 Adaptive Reuse and Community Engagement:



Adaptive reuse is a valuable strategy for addressing the issue of abandoned or underutilized properties, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. However, the success of adaptive reuse projects is not solely dependent on the physical transformation of the property, but also on the engagement and involvement of the surrounding community. In this section, we will discuss the techniques for engaging the community in adaptive reuse and their impact on the success of these projects.



#### 16.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Adaptive Reuse



Community engagement plays a crucial role in the success of adaptive reuse projects. It involves actively involving the community in the decision-making process and incorporating their input and feedback into the project. This not only ensures that the project meets the needs and desires of the community, but also fosters a sense of ownership and pride in the project.



One effective technique for engaging the community in adaptive reuse projects is through the use of social media. Platforms such as Facebook, Twitter, and Instagram can be used to share information about the project, gather feedback and suggestions, and promote community involvement. This allows for a more direct and interactive approach to engaging the community, as well as reaching a wider audience.



Another important technique for community engagement in adaptive reuse is through the use of community workshops and forums. These events provide an opportunity for community members to learn about the project, ask questions, and provide input. This not only helps to address any concerns or challenges, but also allows for a more collaborative and inclusive approach to the project.



In addition, involving local schools and educational institutions in the project can also be an effective way to engage the community. This can include partnering with schools for educational programs or involving students in the design and planning process. By involving the younger generation, the project can foster a sense of community pride and ownership for years to come.



Furthermore, utilizing community surveys and polls can also be a useful technique for engaging the community in adaptive reuse projects. These tools allow for a more structured and organized approach to gathering feedback and suggestions from the community. This can also help to identify any potential concerns or challenges that may arise during the project.



Overall, the techniques for engaging the community in adaptive reuse projects are crucial for ensuring the success and sustainability of these projects. By actively involving the community in the decision-making process and incorporating their input and feedback, the project can better meet the needs and desires of the community, foster a sense of ownership and pride, and address any potential challenges or concerns.

In conclusion, community engagement is a crucial aspect of successful adaptive reuse projects. By actively involving the community in the decision-making process and incorporating their input and feedback, we can ensure that the project meets the needs and desires of the community and fosters a sense of ownership and pride in the project. Utilizing various techniques such as social media, community workshops, and involving local schools and organizations can help to effectively engage the community and contribute to the success of the project.





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of brownfield redevelopment and adaptive reuse as a means of sustainable environmental management. We have discussed the challenges and benefits of redeveloping contaminated sites, as well as the various strategies and techniques that can be used to successfully rehabilitate these areas. Through case studies and examples, we have seen how brownfield redevelopment can not only improve the environment, but also revitalize communities and create economic opportunities.



As we move towards a more environmentally conscious society, it is crucial that we continue to prioritize the remediation and reuse of brownfield sites. By doing so, we can reduce the negative impacts of urban sprawl and preserve valuable green spaces. Additionally, brownfield redevelopment can contribute to the creation of more sustainable and resilient cities, as well as promote social equity by providing affordable housing and job opportunities in disadvantaged areas.



In conclusion, brownfield redevelopment and adaptive reuse are essential components of effective environmental management. By incorporating these practices into our urban planning and development, we can create healthier, more sustainable, and more equitable communities for future generations.

## Chapter: Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment



### Introduction:



In recent years, there has been a growing concern for the environment and the impact of human activities on it. This has led to the development of various environmental management practices aimed at mitigating the negative effects of human actions on the environment. One such practice is brownfield redevelopment, which involves the revitalization of contaminated or abandoned sites for new use. This chapter will focus on the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation, exploring the challenges and opportunities that arise when redeveloping contaminated sites with historical significance.



Brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation share a common goal of revitalizing and repurposing underutilized or abandoned properties. However, these two practices often have conflicting objectives and face unique challenges. While brownfield redevelopment aims to remediate and redevelop contaminated sites, historic preservation seeks to protect and preserve the historical significance of a site. This chapter will delve into the complexities of balancing these objectives and finding solutions that benefit both practices.



The chapter will also discuss the various regulations and policies that govern brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation, including the National Historic Preservation Act and the Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation, and Liability Act. These regulations play a crucial role in guiding the decision-making process and ensuring that both practices are carried out in a responsible and sustainable manner.



Furthermore, this chapter will explore the economic, social, and environmental benefits of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation. By repurposing contaminated sites with historical significance, these practices can contribute to economic growth, create job opportunities, and revitalize communities. They also help to reduce urban sprawl and promote sustainable land use.



Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation. It will highlight the challenges, opportunities, and benefits of these practices and emphasize the importance of responsible and sustainable environmental management. 





### Section: 17.1 Historic Preservation and Brownfields:



Brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation are two practices that share a common goal of revitalizing and repurposing underutilized or abandoned properties. However, these practices often have conflicting objectives and face unique challenges. In this section, we will explore the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation, and the complexities of balancing their objectives.



#### 17.1a Definition of Historic Preservation



Historic preservation, also known as built heritage preservation or built heritage conservation, is an endeavor that seeks to preserve, conserve, and protect buildings, objects, landscapes, or other artifacts of historical significance. It is a philosophical concept that became popular in the twentieth century, which maintains that cities, as products of centuries' development, should be obligated to protect their patrimonial legacy. The term refers specifically to the preservation of the built environment, and not to preservation of, for example, primeval forests or wilderness.



## Areas of Professional, Paid Practice



Paid work in historic preservation can be divided into several practice areas, including regulatory compliance, architecture and construction, historic sites/museums, advocacy, and downtown revitalization/rejuvenation. Each of these areas requires a different set of skills, knowledge, and abilities.



### United States



In the United States, the majority of professional, paid practice in historic preservation falls under the category of regulatory compliance. This area is driven by laws, regulations, and guidelines at the federal, state, and local levels. At the federal level, these include the National Historic Preservation Act of 1966 and associated regulations, such as Section 106 (54 USC 306108, formerly known as 36 CFR 800), National Register of Historic Places (54 USC 302101-302108), and the Secretary of the Interior's Standards (36 CFR 67). Many states have laws that reference these federal regulations or create parallel regulations using similar language. At the local level, preservation laws and regulations are known as "preservation ordinances" and require private property owners to seek a "certificate of appropriateness" when making modifications to existing buildings listed in a local historical register.



### Brownfield Redevelopment and Historic Preservation



Brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation share a common goal of revitalizing and repurposing underutilized or abandoned properties. However, these practices also have conflicting objectives. While brownfield redevelopment aims to remediate and redevelop contaminated sites, historic preservation seeks to protect and preserve the historical significance of a site. This can create challenges when redeveloping brownfields with historical significance, as the remediation process may damage or destroy important historical features.



To address these challenges, it is essential to have a thorough understanding of the site's history and potential historical significance before beginning the remediation process. This can involve conducting historical research, consulting with local historical societies or preservation organizations, and involving stakeholders in the decision-making process. By taking these steps, it is possible to find solutions that balance the objectives of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation.



### Regulations and Policies



Several regulations and policies govern brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation in the United States. These include the National Historic Preservation Act and the Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation, and Liability Act (CERCLA). The National Historic Preservation Act requires federal agencies to take into account the effects of their actions on historic properties and to consult with state historic preservation offices and other stakeholders. CERCLA, also known as the Superfund Act, provides a framework for the cleanup of contaminated sites and assigns liability for the costs of remediation.



These regulations play a crucial role in guiding the decision-making process and ensuring that both practices are carried out in a responsible and sustainable manner. They also help to protect the historical significance of a site while promoting the redevelopment of brownfields.



### Benefits of Brownfield Redevelopment and Historic Preservation



Brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation offer numerous benefits, including economic, social, and environmental benefits. By repurposing contaminated sites with historical significance, these practices can contribute to economic growth, create job opportunities, and revitalize communities. They also help to preserve and celebrate a community's history and cultural heritage, promoting a sense of pride and identity.



Furthermore, brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation can have positive environmental impacts. By redeveloping contaminated sites, these practices can prevent the spread of pollutants and promote the reuse of existing infrastructure, reducing the need for new development on undeveloped land. This can help to protect natural habitats and promote sustainable development practices.



In conclusion, the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation presents unique challenges, but also offers numerous benefits. By balancing the objectives of these practices and following regulations and policies, it is possible to revitalize contaminated sites while preserving their historical significance. This can lead to positive economic, social, and environmental outcomes for communities. 





### Section: 17.1 Historic Preservation and Brownfields:



Brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation are two practices that share a common goal of revitalizing and repurposing underutilized or abandoned properties. However, these practices often have conflicting objectives and face unique challenges. In this section, we will explore the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation, and the complexities of balancing their objectives.



#### 17.1a Definition of Historic Preservation



Historic preservation, also known as built heritage preservation or built heritage conservation, is an endeavor that seeks to preserve, conserve, and protect buildings, objects, landscapes, or other artifacts of historical significance. It is a philosophical concept that became popular in the twentieth century, which maintains that cities, as products of centuries' development, should be obligated to protect their patrimonial legacy. The term refers specifically to the preservation of the built environment, and not to preservation of, for example, primeval forests or wilderness.



Historic preservation is a multi-faceted practice that involves a variety of professionals and stakeholders. It encompasses not only the physical preservation of buildings and structures, but also the preservation of cultural and social significance. This includes the preservation of cultural traditions, stories, and memories associated with a particular place or building. Historic preservation also plays a crucial role in maintaining a sense of community and identity, as well as promoting tourism and economic development.



## Areas of Professional, Paid Practice



Paid work in historic preservation can be divided into several practice areas, including regulatory compliance, architecture and construction, historic sites/museums, advocacy, and downtown revitalization/rejuvenation. ### United States



In the United States, the majority of professional, paid practice in historic preservation falls under the category of regulatory compliance. This area is driven by laws, regulations, and guidelines at the federal, state, and local levels. At the federal level, these include the National Historic Preservation Act of 1966 and associated regulations, such as Section 106 (54 USC 306108, formerly known as 36 CFR 800), National Register of Historic Places (54 USC 302101-302108), and the Secretary of the Interior's Standards (36 CFR 6



### Section: 17.1 Historic Preservation and Brownfields:



#### 17.1b Role of Historic Preservation in Brownfield Redevelopment



The role of historic preservation in brownfield redevelopment is a complex and often contentious issue. On one hand, historic preservation can provide valuable insights and resources for the redevelopment of brownfield sites. On the other hand, the goals of historic preservation and brownfield redevelopment may conflict, leading to challenges and obstacles in the redevelopment process.



One of the main benefits of incorporating historic preservation into brownfield redevelopment is the potential for adaptive reuse. As mentioned in the related context, adaptive building is the process of repurposing old buildings for new uses, which can be more environmentally sustainable and economically friendly than building new structures. Historic preservationists can provide expertise in identifying and preserving the historical and architectural significance of a building, while also finding ways to adapt it for modern use.



In addition, historic preservation can also play a role in mitigating environmental hazards on brownfield sites. By preserving and repurposing existing buildings, the need for demolition and new construction is reduced, which can help prevent further contamination of the site. Historic preservationists can also work with environmental experts to ensure that any necessary remediation efforts are carried out in a way that preserves the historical integrity of the site.



However, there are also challenges and conflicts that can arise when trying to incorporate historic preservation into brownfield redevelopment. One of the main challenges is balancing the goals of historic preservation with the goals of brownfield redevelopment. While historic preservation seeks to preserve the past, brownfield redevelopment is focused on revitalizing and repurposing underutilized or abandoned properties for modern use. This can lead to conflicts over the level of preservation that is necessary or appropriate for a particular site.



Another challenge is the potential for increased costs and delays in the redevelopment process. Historic preservation can be a time-consuming and expensive process, as it often involves extensive research, documentation, and review processes. This can be a barrier for developers who are looking to quickly and efficiently redevelop a brownfield site. In addition, the presence of historical artifacts or structures on a site may require additional remediation efforts, which can add to the overall cost of the project.



Despite these challenges, the role of historic preservation in brownfield redevelopment is an important one. By incorporating historic preservation into the redevelopment process, we can not only revitalize and repurpose underutilized properties, but also preserve our cultural heritage and promote sustainable development practices. It is crucial for all stakeholders involved in brownfield redevelopment to work together to find a balance between historic preservation and redevelopment goals, in order to create successful and sustainable projects.





### Section: 17.1 Historic Preservation and Brownfields:



Brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation are two practices that share a common goal of revitalizing and repurposing underutilized or abandoned properties. However, these practices often have conflicting objectives and face unique challenges. In this section, we will explore the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation, and the complexities of balancing their objectives.



#### 17.1a Definition of Historic Preservation



Historic preservation, also known as built heritage preservation or built heritage conservation, is an endeavor that seeks to preserve, conserve, and protect buildings, objects, landscapes, or other artifacts of historical significance. It is a philosophical concept that became popular in the twentieth century, which maintains that cities, as products of centuries' development, should be obligated to protect their patrimonial legacy. The term refers specifically to the preservation of the built environment, and not to preservation of, for example, primeval forests or wilderness.



Historic preservation is a multi-faceted practice that involves a variety of professionals and stakeholders. It encompasses not only the physical preservation of buildings and structures, but also the preservation of cultural and social significance. This includes the preservation of cultural traditions, stories, and memories associated with a particular place or building. Historic preservation also plays a crucial role in maintaining a sense of community and identity, as well as promoting tourism and economic development.



#### 17.1b Definition of Brownfield Redevelopment



Brownfield redevelopment is the process of revitalizing and redeveloping abandoned or underutilized properties that are contaminated or perceived to be contaminated with hazardous substances, pollutants, or contaminants. These properties, known as brownfields, often have a negative impact on the surrounding environment and community. The Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) defines a brownfield as "a property, the expansion, redevelopment, or reuse of which may be complicated by the presence or potential presence of a hazardous substance, pollutant, or contaminant."



Brownfield redevelopment is a complex and challenging process that requires collaboration between various stakeholders, including government agencies, developers, and community members. The goal of brownfield redevelopment is to transform these contaminated properties into productive and sustainable assets for the community.



#### 17.1c Case Studies in Historic Preservation and Brownfield Redevelopment



To better understand the intersection of historic preservation and brownfield redevelopment, let's examine some case studies where these two practices have been successfully integrated.



One example is the redevelopment of the former Bethlehem Steel plant in Bethlehem, Pennsylvania. This site, which was once the second-largest steel producer in the United States, had been abandoned for over a decade and was heavily contaminated with hazardous substances. However, through a partnership between the city, state, and private developers, the site was transformed into a mixed-use development that includes residential, commercial, and recreational spaces. The project also incorporated the preservation of several historic buildings, including the iconic blast furnaces, which now serve as a museum and event space.



Another successful case study is the redevelopment of the former Navy Yard in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania. This site, which was once a bustling shipyard, had been abandoned for over a decade and was heavily contaminated with hazardous substances. Through a public-private partnership, the site was transformed into a sustainable business campus that includes office spaces, research facilities, and recreational areas. The project also incorporated the preservation of several historic buildings, including the iconic Navy Yard Hospital, which now serves as a mixed-use building with office and retail spaces.



These case studies demonstrate the potential for successful integration of historic preservation and brownfield redevelopment. By preserving the cultural and historical significance of these sites while also addressing environmental concerns, these projects have revitalized the surrounding communities and contributed to sustainable development. 





### Section: 17.2 Historic Preservation Strategies:



Historic preservation strategies are essential for the successful redevelopment of brownfield sites. These strategies involve a combination of regulatory compliance, architectural and construction techniques, and community engagement. In this section, we will explore the various strategies used in historic preservation and their application in brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 17.2a Overview of Historic Preservation Strategies



Historic preservation strategies can be broadly categorized into three main areas: regulatory compliance, architectural and construction techniques, and community engagement. Each of these areas plays a crucial role in preserving the historical significance of a site while also addressing the challenges of brownfield redevelopment.



##### Regulatory Compliance



Regulatory compliance is a critical aspect of historic preservation, particularly in the United States, where about 90% of professional, paid practice in this field is focused on regulatory compliance. This involves adhering to laws, regulations, and guidelines at the federal, state, and local levels. These regulations aim to protect and preserve historic sites and buildings, ensuring that any redevelopment activities do not harm their historical significance.



One example of regulatory compliance in historic preservation is the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA) of 1966 in the United States. This law requires federal agencies to take into account the effects of their actions on historic properties and to consult with the State Historic Preservation Officer (SHPO) and the Advisory Council on Historic Preservation (ACHP) before undertaking any projects that could potentially harm historic sites.



##### Architectural and Construction Techniques



Architectural and construction techniques play a crucial role in preserving the physical integrity of historic buildings and structures. These techniques involve using specialized materials and methods to repair and restore historic structures while maintaining their original design and character.



One example of an architectural technique used in historic preservation is the concept of "adaptive reuse." This involves repurposing a historic building for a new use while preserving its original features. For example, an old factory could be converted into a mixed-use development with residential and commercial spaces, while still retaining its original facade and industrial charm.



##### Community Engagement



Community engagement is a vital aspect of historic preservation, as it involves the participation and support of local communities in the preservation process. This can include educating the public about the historical significance of a site, involving community members in decision-making processes, and promoting community ownership and pride in historic sites.



One example of community engagement in historic preservation is the use of historic districts. These are designated areas that contain a concentration of historic buildings and structures. By designating a district as historic, the community is involved in preserving the area's character and promoting its historical significance.



In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement is crucial in gaining support and buy-in from local residents for the revitalization of contaminated sites. By involving the community in the preservation process, stakeholders can work together to find a balance between preserving the historical significance of a site and addressing the environmental challenges of brownfield redevelopment.



In the next section, we will explore specific historic preservation strategies and their application in brownfield redevelopment projects. 





### Section: 17.2 Historic Preservation Strategies:



Historic preservation strategies are essential for the successful redevelopment of brownfield sites. These strategies involve a combination of regulatory compliance, architectural and construction techniques, and community engagement. In this section, we will explore the various strategies used in historic preservation and their application in brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 17.2a Overview of Historic Preservation Strategies



Historic preservation strategies can be broadly categorized into three main areas: regulatory compliance, architectural and construction techniques, and community engagement. Each of these areas plays a crucial role in preserving the historical significance of a site while also addressing the challenges of brownfield redevelopment.



##### Regulatory Compliance



Regulatory compliance is a critical aspect of historic preservation, particularly in the United States, where about 90% of professional, paid practice in this field is focused on regulatory compliance. This involves adhering to laws, regulations, and guidelines at the federal, state, and local levels. These regulations aim to protect and preserve historic sites and buildings, ensuring that any redevelopment activities do not harm their historical significance.



One example of regulatory compliance in historic preservation is the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA) of 1966 in the United States. This law requires federal agencies to take into account the effects of their actions on historic properties and to consult with the State Historic Preservation Officer (SHPO) and the Advisory Council on Historic Preservation (ACHP) before undertaking any projects that could potentially harm historic sites.



##### Architectural and Construction Techniques



Architectural and construction techniques play a crucial role in preserving the physical integrity of historic buildings and structures. These techniques involve using specialized materials and methods to repair and restore historic structures while maintaining their original character. For example, when repairing a historic brick building, it is important to use traditional lime mortar instead of modern cement-based mortar, as it allows the building to "breathe" and prevents moisture damage.



Another important aspect of architectural and construction techniques in historic preservation is the use of adaptive reuse. This involves repurposing a historic building for a new function while still preserving its historical features. For example, an old factory building can be converted into a mixed-use development with apartments, offices, and retail spaces, while still retaining its original facade and industrial character.



##### Community Engagement



Community engagement is a crucial aspect of historic preservation, as it involves working with local communities to identify and preserve their cultural heritage. This can include educating the public about the historical significance of a site, involving community members in the decision-making process for redevelopment projects, and promoting community involvement in the maintenance and upkeep of historic sites.



One example of community engagement in historic preservation is the use of heritage tourism. This involves promoting historic sites as tourist destinations, which not only generates revenue for the local community but also raises awareness and appreciation for the site's historical significance.



#### 17.2b Selection of Historic Preservation Strategies



The selection of historic preservation strategies for a brownfield redevelopment project depends on various factors, including the site's historical significance, the type of contamination present, and the intended use of the site after redevelopment. It is essential to conduct a thorough assessment of these factors to determine the most appropriate strategies for each project.



One approach to selecting historic preservation strategies is the use of a preservation plan. This plan outlines the goals and objectives for preserving the site's historical significance and identifies the specific strategies that will be used to achieve these goals. The plan should also include a timeline and budget for implementing these strategies.



Another important consideration in the selection of historic preservation strategies is the involvement of stakeholders. This includes not only the local community but also government agencies, developers, and other interested parties. By involving stakeholders in the decision-making process, a more comprehensive and inclusive preservation plan can be developed, leading to a more successful and sustainable brownfield redevelopment project.



In conclusion, historic preservation strategies are crucial for the successful redevelopment of brownfield sites. By combining regulatory compliance, architectural and construction techniques, and community engagement, these strategies can help preserve the historical significance of a site while also addressing the challenges of brownfield redevelopment. The selection of these strategies should be carefully considered and involve the input of stakeholders to ensure the best possible outcome for both the site and the community.





### Section: 17.2 Historic Preservation Strategies:



Historic preservation strategies are essential for the successful redevelopment of brownfield sites. These strategies involve a combination of regulatory compliance, architectural and construction techniques, and community engagement. In this section, we will explore the various strategies used in historic preservation and their application in brownfield redevelopment projects.



#### 17.2a Overview of Historic Preservation Strategies



Historic preservation strategies can be broadly categorized into three main areas: regulatory compliance, architectural and construction techniques, and community engagement. Each of these areas plays a crucial role in preserving the historical significance of a site while also addressing the challenges of brownfield redevelopment.



##### Regulatory Compliance



Regulatory compliance is a critical aspect of historic preservation, particularly in the United States, where about 90% of professional, paid practice in this field is focused on regulatory compliance. This involves adhering to laws, regulations, and guidelines at the federal, state, and local levels. These regulations aim to protect and preserve historic sites and buildings, ensuring that any redevelopment activities do not harm their historical significance.



One example of regulatory compliance in historic preservation is the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA) of 1966 in the United States. This law requires federal agencies to take into account the effects of their actions on historic properties and to consult with the State Historic Preservation Officer (SHPO) and the Advisory Council on Historic Preservation (ACHP) before undertaking any projects that could potentially harm historic sites.



##### Architectural and Construction Techniques



Architectural and construction techniques play a crucial role in preserving the physical integrity of historic buildings and structures. These techniques involve using specialized materials and methods that are sensitive to the historical significance of the site. For example, when renovating a historic building, it is important to use materials that are similar to the original ones, such as using traditional brick and mortar instead of modern cement. This helps to maintain the authenticity and character of the building.



In addition, construction techniques must also be carefully planned and executed to avoid damaging the historical features of the site. This may involve using non-intrusive methods, such as hand tools, to minimize the impact on the building. It may also involve reinforcing or stabilizing the structure to prevent further deterioration.



##### Community Engagement



Community engagement is a crucial aspect of historic preservation strategies. It involves actively involving the local community in the decision-making process and encouraging their participation in the preservation and redevelopment of historic sites. This not only helps to build support for the project but also ensures that the community's values and perspectives are taken into account.



One example of community engagement in historic preservation is the use of public forums and workshops to gather input from community members. This can help to identify the community's priorities and concerns, which can then be incorporated into the preservation and redevelopment plans.



#### 17.2b Case Studies in Historic Preservation Strategies



To further illustrate the application of historic preservation strategies in brownfield redevelopment, let us examine some case studies.



##### Hewitt & Emerson



One notable case study is the Hewitt & Emerson project, which involved the adaptive reuse of a historic building in downtown Boston. The project team worked closely with regulatory agencies to ensure compliance with historic preservation laws and guidelines. They also used specialized construction techniques to preserve the building's historical features, such as restoring the original windows and using traditional materials for repairs.



In addition, the project team engaged with the local community through public forums and workshops to gather input and address any concerns. This helped to build support for the project and ensure that the community's values were taken into account.



##### Ribeirinha (Horta)



Another case study is the Ribeirinha project in Horta, Portugal. This project involved the preservation and adaptive reuse of a group of architecturally significant buildings that had been affected by earthquakes and inundation. The project team used a combination of regulatory compliance, architectural and construction techniques, and community engagement to successfully preserve and redevelop the site.



Through regulatory compliance, the project team ensured that the historical significance of the buildings was not compromised. They also used specialized construction techniques to reinforce and stabilize the structures, while also preserving their architectural integrity. Community engagement was also a key aspect of this project, as the team worked closely with local stakeholders to gather input and address any concerns.



#### 17.2c Case Studies in Historic Preservation Strategies



To further explore the application of historic preservation strategies, let us examine some case studies in the context of urban regeneration.



##### Indigenous Architecture



In Canada, there has been a growing movement towards incorporating indigenous architecture into urban regeneration projects. This involves using traditional building techniques and materials, as well as incorporating indigenous cultural values and perspectives into the design process. This not only helps to preserve and celebrate indigenous heritage but also promotes sustainable development and cultural diversity in urban areas.



##### Adaptive Reuse and Built Heritage Preservation



In many urban areas, there is a growing trend towards adaptive reuse of historic buildings for other purposes. This not only helps to preserve the physical integrity of the buildings but also promotes sustainable development by repurposing existing structures instead of building new ones. In addition, adaptive reuse can also contribute to the unique character and competitiveness of a town or city.



In conclusion, historic preservation strategies are crucial for the successful redevelopment of brownfield sites. By adhering to regulatory compliance, using specialized architectural and construction techniques, and engaging with the local community, we can ensure that the historical significance of these sites is preserved while also promoting sustainable development and cultural diversity. 





### Section: 17.3 Historic Preservation and Community Engagement:



Historic preservation and community engagement go hand in hand when it comes to the successful redevelopment of brownfield sites. In this section, we will explore the role of community engagement in historic preservation and how it can contribute to the overall success of a brownfield redevelopment project.



#### 17.3a Role of Community Engagement in Historic Preservation



Community engagement is a crucial aspect of historic preservation as it allows for the involvement of local communities in the decision-making process. This is especially important when it comes to brownfield redevelopment, as these sites often have a significant impact on the surrounding communities.



One of the main benefits of community engagement in historic preservation is the inclusion of diverse perspectives and values. Local communities have a deep connection to their heritage and can provide valuable insights into the significance of a cultural place or site. This is in line with the concept of "value-led conservation," which emphasizes the importance of understanding stakeholders and their associated values in heritage management.



Moreover, community engagement can also help in identifying potential threats to historic sites and finding sustainable solutions. By involving local communities, conservation efforts can be tailored to meet the specific needs and concerns of the community. This can lead to more effective and long-term preservation of historic sites.



In addition, community engagement can also serve as a means of education and knowledge exchange. By involving local communities in the preservation process, they can learn about the history and significance of the site and become advocates for its protection. This can also lead to increased awareness and appreciation for cultural heritage in the community.



Examples of successful community engagement in historic preservation can be seen in various projects around the world. In Homs, Syria, local charities have been working with the community to protect their heritage, while in Dangeil, Sudan, a conservation program has used social and economic relationships with the community to ensure the sustainability of the project.



In Australia, Indigenous communities have also taken on stewardship of conservation and management programs for their cultural heritage places and landscapes, particularly those containing rock art. This not only allows for the preservation of their heritage but also empowers these communities to take ownership and responsibility for their cultural sites.



In conclusion, community engagement plays a vital role in historic preservation and should be an integral part of any brownfield redevelopment project. By involving local communities, we can ensure the preservation of cultural heritage while also promoting sustainable and inclusive development. 





### Section: 17.3 Historic Preservation and Community Engagement:



Historic preservation and community engagement are essential components of successful brownfield redevelopment projects. In this section, we will explore the techniques for engaging the community in historic preservation and how it can contribute to the overall success of a brownfield redevelopment project.



#### 17.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Historic Preservation



Community engagement is a crucial aspect of historic preservation as it allows for the involvement of local communities in the decision-making process. This is especially important when it comes to brownfield redevelopment, as these sites often have a significant impact on the surrounding communities.



One technique for engaging the community in historic preservation is through participatory action research (PAR). PAR is a method used by archaeologists in community archaeology projects, where community members are actively involved in the research process. This approach allows for the inclusion of diverse perspectives and values, as well as the identification of potential threats to historic sites.



Another technique is through community archaeology, where community members are involved in the excavation and preservation of archaeological sites. This approach not only allows for community engagement but also serves as a means of education and knowledge exchange. By involving community members in the preservation process, they can learn about the history and significance of the site and become advocates for its protection.



In addition, the use of digital technology in the field of archaeology has also allowed for greater community engagement in historic preservation. The use of 3D modeling technology, for example, allows for the accurate modeling of archaeological sites, providing further information to formulate archaeological perspectives and promoting communication between the cultural heritage of archaeological sites and the public population.



Furthermore, community engagement can also be achieved through the use of online courses and resources, such as the Post Carbon Institute's "Think Resilience" course. This course teaches individuals how to make sense of complex challenges and build community resilience, which can be applied to historic preservation efforts.



Overall, community engagement in historic preservation is crucial for the successful redevelopment of brownfield sites. By involving local communities, conservation efforts can be tailored to meet the specific needs and concerns of the community, leading to more effective and sustainable preservation of historic sites. 





### Section: 17.3 Historic Preservation and Community Engagement:



Historic preservation and community engagement are essential components of successful brownfield redevelopment projects. In this section, we will explore the techniques for engaging the community in historic preservation and how it can contribute to the overall success of a brownfield redevelopment project.



#### 17.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Historic Preservation



Community engagement is a crucial aspect of historic preservation as it allows for the involvement of local communities in the decision-making process. This is especially important when it comes to brownfield redevelopment, as these sites often have a significant impact on the surrounding communities.



One technique for engaging the community in historic preservation is through participatory action research (PAR). PAR is a method used by archaeologists in community archaeology projects, where community members are actively involved in the research process. This approach allows for the inclusion of diverse perspectives and values, as well as the identification of potential threats to historic sites.



Another technique is through community archaeology, where community members are involved in the excavation and preservation of archaeological sites. This approach not only allows for community engagement but also serves as a means of education and knowledge exchange. By involving community members in the preservation process, they can learn about the history and significance of the site and become advocates for its protection.



In addition, the use of digital technology in the field of archaeology has also allowed for greater community engagement in historic preservation. The use of 3D modeling technology, for example, allows for the accurate modeling of archaeological sites, providing further information to formulate archaeological perspectives and promoting communication between the cultural heritage of archaeological sites and modern communities.



#### 17.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Historic Preservation



To further illustrate the importance and effectiveness of community engagement in historic preservation, let's take a look at some case studies.



One notable project is the Center for Public History and Digital Humanities at Midwestern U.S. This center focuses on engaging the community in preserving and promoting the history and cultural heritage of the region through various digital platforms and public events. By involving the community in the preservation process, the center has been able to create a sense of ownership and pride in the local history and promote community resilience.



Another example is the Post Carbon Institute's Think Resilience online course. This course aims to educate individuals and communities on how to build resilience in the face of complex challenges, including the preservation of historic sites. By engaging the community in discussions and activities related to historic preservation, the course promotes a deeper understanding and appreciation for the importance of preserving our cultural heritage.



Lastly, the Settlers, Rails & Trails project in Canada showcases the successful collaboration between indigenous communities and architects in preserving and promoting contemporary indigenous architecture. By involving the community in the design and construction process, the project not only preserves the cultural heritage of indigenous communities but also promotes cultural exchange and understanding.



In conclusion, community engagement is a crucial aspect of historic preservation and plays a significant role in the success of brownfield redevelopment projects. By involving the community in the preservation process, we not only ensure the protection of our cultural heritage but also promote education, understanding, and resilience within our communities. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation. We have seen how these two fields can work together to revitalize and repurpose abandoned or contaminated sites while also preserving their historical significance. We have discussed the challenges and opportunities that arise when redeveloping brownfields with historic structures, and the importance of considering both environmental and cultural factors in the planning and execution of these projects.



One key takeaway from this chapter is the importance of collaboration and communication between environmental management professionals and historic preservationists. By working together, these two groups can ensure that brownfield redevelopment projects are both environmentally sustainable and culturally sensitive. This collaboration can also lead to innovative solutions and creative approaches to revitalizing these sites.



Another important aspect of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation is the potential for economic and social benefits. By repurposing abandoned or underutilized sites, we can create new opportunities for economic growth and community development. Additionally, preserving historic structures can help maintain a sense of identity and pride within a community.



Overall, brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation are complex and multifaceted fields that require careful consideration and collaboration. By understanding the intersection of these two areas and working together, we can create sustainable and culturally sensitive solutions for redeveloping brownfields.

## Chapter: Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment



### Introduction:



Brownfield redevelopment is a crucial aspect of environmental management, as it involves the revitalization of contaminated or abandoned sites for productive use. These sites, known as brownfields, pose significant environmental and health risks to surrounding communities. However, with proper management and remediation, brownfields can be transformed into valuable assets, contributing to economic growth and sustainable development.



In this chapter, we will focus on the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and food systems. Food systems refer to the processes and infrastructure involved in producing, distributing, and consuming food. Brownfield redevelopment can have a significant impact on food systems, as it can provide new opportunities for urban agriculture, food production, and access to fresh, healthy food in underserved communities.



We will explore the challenges and opportunities of incorporating food systems into brownfield redevelopment projects. This includes addressing potential contamination issues, designing sustainable and resilient food systems, and engaging with local communities to ensure equitable access to food resources. Additionally, we will discuss case studies and best practices from successful brownfield redevelopment projects that have integrated food systems.



By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of the potential benefits and challenges of incorporating food systems into brownfield redevelopment. This knowledge can inform future projects and policies, promoting sustainable and equitable development in communities. 





### Section: 18.1 Food Systems and Brownfields:



Brownfield redevelopment is a crucial aspect of environmental management, as it involves the revitalization of contaminated or abandoned sites for productive use. These sites, known as brownfields, pose significant environmental and health risks to surrounding communities. However, with proper management and remediation, brownfields can be transformed into valuable assets, contributing to economic growth and sustainable development.



In this section, we will explore the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and food systems. Food systems refer to the processes and infrastructure involved in producing, distributing, and consuming food. Brownfield redevelopment can have a significant impact on food systems, as it can provide new opportunities for urban agriculture, food production, and access to fresh, healthy food in underserved communities.



#### 18.1a Definition of Food Systems



The term "food system" encompasses the interconnected systems and processes that influence nutrition, food, health, community development, and agriculture. It includes all processes and infrastructure involved in feeding a population, from growing and harvesting to processing, packaging, transporting, marketing, consumption, distribution, and disposal of food and food-related items. This definition falls within the broader concept of agri-food systems, which encompasses the entire range of actors and their interlinked value-adding activities in the primary production of food and non-food agricultural products.



Food systems operate within and are influenced by social, political, economic, technological, and environmental contexts. They also require human resources that provide labor, research, and education. Food systems can be categorized as either conventional or alternative, depending on their model of food lifespan from origin to plate. Additionally, food systems are dependent on a multitude of ecosystem services, such as natural pest regulation, microorganisms providing nitrogen-fixation, and pollinators.



According to the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), the global food system, including all of the various industries involved in sustainable and conventional food systems, provides employment for 1 billion people. However, this global food system is facing a number of challenges, including impeding global food security issues created by climate change and non-climate change stresses on the system. In fact, about 34% of total greenhouse gas emissions are attributable to the global food system. A 2020 EU evidence review found that food system gas emissions are on course to increase by 30-40% by 2050 due to population growth and dietary changes. Therefore, it is crucial to build the resilience of agrifood systems to mitigate these challenges and promote sustainable development.



In the next section, we will discuss the challenges and opportunities of incorporating food systems into brownfield redevelopment projects. This includes addressing potential contamination issues, designing sustainable and resilient food systems, and engaging with local communities to ensure equitable access to food resources. We will also explore case studies and best practices from successful brownfield redevelopment projects that have integrated food systems. By the end of this section, readers will have a better understanding of the potential benefits and challenges of incorporating food systems into brownfield redevelopment, which can inform future projects and policies for sustainable and equitable development in communities.





### Section: 18.1 Food Systems and Brownfields:



Brownfield redevelopment is a crucial aspect of environmental management, as it involves the revitalization of contaminated or abandoned sites for productive use. These sites, known as brownfields, pose significant environmental and health risks to surrounding communities. However, with proper management and remediation, brownfields can be transformed into valuable assets, contributing to economic growth and sustainable development.



In this section, we will explore the role of food systems in brownfield redevelopment. Food systems refer to the processes and infrastructure involved in producing, distributing, and consuming food. Brownfield redevelopment can have a significant impact on food systems, as it can provide new opportunities for urban agriculture, food production, and access to fresh, healthy food in underserved communities.



#### 18.1b Role of Food Systems in Brownfield Redevelopment



The role of food systems in brownfield redevelopment is multifaceted. On one hand, brownfield redevelopment can have a positive impact on food systems by providing new opportunities for urban agriculture and food production. By repurposing abandoned or underutilized land, brownfield redevelopment can create space for community gardens, urban farms, and other forms of local food production. This not only increases access to fresh, healthy food in underserved communities, but also promotes sustainable and resilient food systems.



On the other hand, food systems can also play a crucial role in the remediation and redevelopment of brownfields. For example, certain plants have the ability to absorb and break down contaminants in the soil, a process known as phytoremediation. By strategically planting these plants on brownfield sites, food systems can help to naturally clean up the land and make it suitable for future use. This not only reduces the cost and environmental impact of traditional remediation methods, but also creates a more sustainable and environmentally friendly approach to brownfield redevelopment.



Furthermore, food systems can also contribute to the economic viability of brownfield redevelopment projects. By incorporating food production and distribution into the redevelopment plans, these projects can create new jobs and stimulate local economies. This can be especially beneficial in areas that have been economically impacted by the presence of brownfields.



In addition to these direct impacts, food systems can also play a role in promoting community engagement and social equity in brownfield redevelopment. By involving community members in the planning and implementation of food systems on brownfield sites, these projects can foster a sense of ownership and pride in the community. This can also help to address issues of food insecurity and food deserts in underserved communities.



In conclusion, the role of food systems in brownfield redevelopment is crucial and multifaceted. By incorporating food systems into the remediation and redevelopment of brownfield sites, we can create more sustainable, resilient, and equitable communities. This not only benefits the environment and public health, but also promotes economic growth and social well-being. 





### Section: 18.1 Food Systems and Brownfields:



Brownfield redevelopment is a crucial aspect of environmental management, as it involves the revitalization of contaminated or abandoned sites for productive use. These sites, known as brownfields, pose significant environmental and health risks to surrounding communities. However, with proper management and remediation, brownfields can be transformed into valuable assets, contributing to economic growth and sustainable development.



In this section, we will explore the role of food systems in brownfield redevelopment. Food systems refer to the processes and infrastructure involved in producing, distributing, and consuming food. Brownfield redevelopment can have a significant impact on food systems, as it can provide new opportunities for urban agriculture, food production, and access to fresh, healthy food in underserved communities.



#### 18.1b Role of Food Systems in Brownfield Redevelopment

This not only increases access to fresh, healthy food in underserved communities, but also promotes sustainable and resilient food systems.

For example, certain plants have the ability to absorb and break down contaminants in the soil, a process known as phytoremediation.
